        <p class="date">
         Sun Sep 21, 2014 at 12:40 PM PDT
        </p>
        <h2>
         <a href="/story/2014/09/21/1330771/--The-Half-Has-Never-Been-Told-Slavery-and-the-Making-of-American-Capitalism" id="titleHref">
          'The Half Has Never Been Told: Slavery and the Making of American Capitalism'
         </a>
         <span style="white-space: nowrap">
         </span>
        </h2>
        <p class="author">
         <span>
          by
         </span>
         <a href="/user/Susan Grigsby">
          Susan Grigsby
         </a>
         <a alt="Follow this author" class="ajax type-user do-hotlist do-updateContent size-tiny off altTooltip" href="#?friend_id=161474&amp;is_stream=1">
          Follow
         </a>
         <span class="for">
          for
         </span>
         <a href="/blog/main">
          Daily Kos
         </a>
        </p>
        <ul class="front-page-share clearfix">
         <li style="margin-left: 0px">
          <div class="fb-share-button" data-href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/09/21/1330771/--The-Half-Has-Never-Been-Told-Slavery-and-the-Making-of-American-Capitalism" data-layout="button_count">
          </div>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="twitter-share-button" data-count="horizontal" data-related="markos" data-text="'The Half Has Never Been Told: Slavery and the Making of American Capitalism'" data-url="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/09/21/1330771/--The-Half-Has-Never-Been-Told-Slavery-and-the-Making-of-American-Capitalism" data-via="dailykos" href="https://twitter.com/share">
           Tweet
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="comments comment_count_with_new" data-comment_count="217" data-story_id="1330771" href="/story/2014/09/21/1330771/--The-Half-Has-Never-Been-Told-Slavery-and-the-Making-of-American-Capitalism#comments">
           217 Comments
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <div class="article-body">
        <div id="intro">
         <div class="dkimg-c">
          <span class="image_container">
           <img alt="Drawing of a group of slaves in chains, a ship with slaves and the US Capitol in the distance. Caption reads United States slave trade 1830" height="315" src="http://images.dailykos.com/images/106411/large/US_Slave_Trade_1830.jpg?1411006386" width="550"/>
          </span>
         </div>
         On Sept. 4,
         <a href="http://www.economist.com/news/books/21615864-how-slaves-built-american-capitalism-blood-cotton">
          <i>
           The Economist
          </i>
          published a book review
         </a>
         , for which it apologized, retracted, and then posted on the web. It created no end of furor and discussion about whether the book, or the review, was racist. The anonymous reviewer accused the author of not giving slave owners any credit for their "better treatment" of the slaves which may have been the reason that cotton production increased. He also found that "Almost all the blacks in his book are victims, almost all the whites villains." Since the book concerned slavery in America, Twitter went delightfully mad with #economistbookreviews.
         <p>
          It seemed to me that the
          <i>
           Economist
          </i>
          review of
          <em>
           The Half Has Never Been Told: Slavery and the Making of American Capitalism
          </em>
          was wildly successful. Because while we were all busy smirking, grinning and even laughing at the reviewer, the conversation that the book should have provoked, that
          <i>
           The Economist
          </i>
          wanted at all costs to avoid, was never discussed. What did
          <i>
           The Economist
          </i>
          want to avoid talking about?
         </p>
         <p>
          Back wages, perhaps?
         </p>
         <p>
          Or perhaps what caused the Panic of 1837?
         </p>
         <p>
          Or the ingenuity and perseverance of those whose misfortune it was to be enslaved?
         </p>
         <p>
          Or perhaps it was this:
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          The idea that the commodification and suffering and forced labor of African Americans is what made the United States powerful and rich is not an idea that people necessarily are happy to hear. Yet it is the truth.
         </blockquote>
         <br/>
         More truth is below the fold.
        </div>
        <p class="divider-doodle">
        </p>
        <div class="article-body" id="body">
         <div class="dkimg-r">
          <span class="image_container">
           <img alt="Black and white book cover includes photo of cotton field stretching to the horizon. " height="380" src="http://images.dailykos.com/images/106396/small/Half_Has_Never_Been_Told.jpg?1410999690" width="250"/>
          </span>
         </div>
         <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/046500296X/ref=as_li_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=390957&amp;creativeASIN=046500296X&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=daikos-20&amp;linkId=PIWE3C73XJZ4IVCG">
          The Half Has Never Been Told:
          <br/>
          Slavery and the Making of American Capitalism
         </a>
         <br/>
         By Edward E. Baptist
         <br/>
         Published by Basic Books
         <br/>
         September 9, 2014
         <br/>
         528 pages
         <p>
          In
          <em>
           The Half Has Never Been Told
          </em>
          , Edward E. Baptist, author, historian and Cornell University associate professor, artfully combines the individual voices of an enslaved population within a broader discussion of the economic and geographic growth of America in the nineteenth century. But mostly, he paints a picture of unrestrained, laissez-faire capitalism. A form of capitalism undreamt of by any but the most ardent Ayn Rand fans. It is this picture of capitalism unbound that
          <i>
           The Economist
          </i>
          likely does not want us to discuss.
         </p>
         <p>
          In this story of American slavery, and its intimate connection to American capitalism, Baptist demonstrates how the availability of cheap land, slave labor and a government willing to support it can create huge amounts of wealth. Enough wealth to help fund an industrial revolution.
         </p>
         <p>
          Using thousands of WPA-era interviews and hundreds of post-Civil War memoirs and autobiographies written by former slaves, he brings to life the giant that he uses as his metaphor and organizing tool. The image of this giant comes from an essay written by Ralph Ellison:
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          "On the moral level I propose we view the whole of American life as a drama enacted on the body of a Negro giant who, lying trussed up like Gulliver, forms the stage and the scene upon which and within which the action unfolds."
         </blockquote>
         Baptist takes apart the myths that our society has created to make us more comfortable with our slave-owning past. He begins with the biggest myth of all, that slavery was unprofitable, inefficient, and would eventually die off as it would be unable to adapt and to compete with industrialization.
         <p>
          Sure.
         </p>
         <p>
          Unfortunately, there is absolutely no evidence that it was either inefficient or that it was dying out. On the contrary, the cheap and ready availability of stolen lands and easy credit due to creative financial instruments, combined with the slave labor that the laws allowed, encouraged and protected, led to a boom in cotton production that showed little signs of slowing by 1860.
         </p>
         <p>
          In 1860, the Southern slave labor camps provided 88 percent of the cotton used in Great Britain's cotton mills. Cotton had become the number one trading commodity of the entire world. It fueled the industrial revolution, feeding not just the cotton mills of Britain, but also the ones in towns like Lowell, Massachusetts. The cotton mills of Lowell were built with the profits made from the unpaid labor of African Americans in the slave labor camps. Cotton went from 14 percent of the total American exports in 1802 to 61 percent by 1860. The United States share of the worldwide cotton market climbed from one percent in 1801 to 66 percent by 1860.
         </p>
         <p>
          As new lands were opened by the Louisiana Purchase and the forced eviction of almost 50,000 Native Americans from the lands to which they held title, slavery moved from the coastal states of the Old South to the new Southwest. Foreclosed from importing new slaves to work the new land by the Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves of 1807, slave traders forced the migration of one million slaves:
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          From the 1790s to the 1860s, enslavers moved 1 million people from the old slave states to the new. They went from making no cotton to speak of in 1790 to making almost 2 billion pounds of it in 1860. Stretching out beyond the slave South, the story encompassed not only Washington politicians and voters across the United States but also Connecticut factories, London banks, opium addicts in China, and consumers in East Africa.
          <br/>
         </blockquote>
         On the eve of the Civil War, the enslavers were eyeing land in Texas, California, and even Cuba to further the expansion of the slave labor camps. The number of slaves had increased five-fold over what it was in the 1790s.
         <br/>
         <div class="dkimg-c">
          <span class="image_container">
           <img alt="Slaves Waiting for Sale: Richmond, Virginia. Painted upon the sketch of 1853 by Eyre Crowe" height="372" src="http://images.dailykos.com/images/106565/large/Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond__Virginia.jpg?1411072547" width="550"/>
          </span>
          <div class="dkimg-cap">
           Slaves Waiting for Sale: Richmond, Virginia
           <br/>
           Eyre Crowe Painting from sketch of 1853
          </div>
         </div>
         In the vernacular of the African American, this forced migration, this tearing apart of families and homes, was called stealing. A man was "stole" from his home in a Virginia slave labor camp, and shipped down the river to New Orleans to be sold. In calling this a theft, the African American community was identifying the criminal nature of slavery and building a common story of themselves, as a people.
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          The crop of cotton in 1859 was astonishing almost 2 billion pounds of clean fiber in 4 million bales. Slaverys productivity was higher than ever some 700 pounds per enslaved man, woman, and child in the cotton country, twenty-two times the rate in 1790.
         </blockquote>
         The astounding increase in productivity was due to the ingenuity of the African American slaves; not because they were treated so well by their enslavers, but because they faced whippings, and worse, should they fail to meet their quotas. So they found ways to pick cotton with both hands, to pick it faster and to waste less motion in order to avoid the whip's lash. The quotas were increased regularly, creating a push system of labor, requiring ever-increasing productivity from the enslaved.
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          And thus untold amounts of mental labor, unknown breakthroughs of human creativity, were the keys to an astonishing increase in cotton production that required no machinery save the whipping-machine, of course. With it, enslavers looted the riches of black folks minds, stole days and months and years and lifetimes, turned sweat, blood, and flesh into gold.
         </blockquote>
         According to Baptist, during the Civil War, when demand for cotton far exceeded the supply, the federal government leased land on South Carolina's Sea Islands to northern entrepreneurs who would attempt to grow cotton using employees who were paid for their labor. Despite the experiments that included monthly wages and wages paid per pound of cotton picked, they were unable to achieve anything near the productivity of the slave labor camps.
         <p>
          <em>
           The Half Has Never Been Told
          </em>
          is a story that covers an immense amount of territory. Baptist includes the individual narratives of the enslaved people and letters from those whose wealth depended on the slave trade or slave labor. It is from one of those letters that we learn of the casualness with which women's sexual slavery is discussed. "Fancy" girls were sold for a price directly related to her light skin and sexual attractiveness.
         </p>
         <p>
          Baptist also examines the tendency toward violence that was prevalent in the South as white men, in order to prove they were superior to the enslaved men, took offense at the any perceived slight to their honor. Duels to defend that honor distinguished them from the African American who, of course, was not allowed to defend himself or his loved ones.
         </p>
         <p>
          He draws other parallels to today while exploring the causes of the Panic of 1837, including the lessening of financial regulations, the creation of new financial instruments and the belief that cotton prices must always rise in defiance of the laws of supply and demand.
         </p>
         <p>
          An excerpt from
          <em>
           The Half Has Never Been Told
          </em>
          was published by Salon, and can be found
          <a href="http://www.salon.com/2014/09/07/we_still_lie_about_slavery_heres_the_truth_about_how_the_american_economy_and_power_were_built_on_forced_migration_and_torture/">
           online
          </a>
          .
         </p>
         <p>
          If unregulated capitalism succeeds wildly only through theft and torture, should we not be regulating it more closely? Should we not take steps to insure that the theft of wages is never again allowed to happen?
         </p>
         <p>
          And if the Koch brothers, and the Waltons, and so many other Americans can live off of the wealth created by their ancestors, isn't something owing to the heirs of the African Americans who created the wealth of this nation?
         </p>
         <p>
          Perhaps this is what
          <i>
           The Economist
          </i>
          is hoping we won't talk about, being distracted as we are, by a review that suggests that reality has a liberal bias.
          <br/>
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
       <h4>
        Originally posted to
        <a href="/blog/main/">
         Daily Kos
        </a>
        on Sun Sep 21, 2014 at 12:40 PM PDT.
       </h4>
       <h4>
        Also republished by
        <a href="/blog/Black Kos community/">
         Black Kos community
        </a>
        .
       </h4>

         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">
          <p>
           <b>
            More Tagging tips:
           </b>
          </p>
          <p>
           A tag is a way to search for this diary. If someone is searching for "Barack Obama," is this a diary they'd be trying to find?
          </p>
          <p>
           Use a person's full name, without any title. Senator Obama may become President Obama, and Michelle Obama might run for office.
          </p>
          <p>
           If your diary covers an election or elected official, use election tags, which are generally the state abbreviation followed by the office. 
		CA-01 is the first district House seat. CA-Sen covers both senate races. NY-GOV covers the New York governor's race.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tags do not compound: that is, "education reform" is a completely different tag from "education". A tag like "reform" alone is probably not meaningful.
          </p>
         </div>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             unlike current globalization, de jure slavery ended but de facto slavery remained - simply subcontracted around the Empire
             <br/>
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             It is believed that after 1833 clandestine slave-trading continued within the British Empire; in 1854 Nathaniel Isaacs, owner of the island of Matakong off the coast of Sierra Leone was accused of slave-trading by the governor of Sierra Leone, Sir Arthur Kennedy. Papers relating to the charges were lost when the Forerunner was wrecked off Madeira in October 1854. In the absence of the papers, the English courts refused to proceed with the prosecution.
             <p>
              In Australia, blackbirding and the holding of indigenous workers' pay "in trust" continued, in some instances into the 1970s.
             </p>
            </blockquote>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             The law that banned slavery in all of the British Empire's colonies did not pass until
             <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833">
              1833.
             </a>
             <br/>
            </p>

            <p class="sig">

             I don't know the exact years
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">

              12+ / 0-

           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Obviously you can google it in a few seconds. But I know that it was before the US did. But if you purchase and profit off of products made by the slaves of others, what's the difference, really? You can't have it both ways. Slavery entailed a division of labor. Some used slaves to product raw materials, and others processed it and sold the finished products.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             "We have it in our power to begin the world over again." - Thomas Paine, Common Sense, 1776
            </p>
            <p class="cb">
             by
             <a href="/user/uid:65471">
              kovie
             </a>
             on
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54550319#c15">

         <ul class="cm i1" id="k54550319">
          <li id="c92">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54551214">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              There is clearly a difference between people who
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54551214#c92?mode=alone;showrate=1#c92">
               2+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:107837">
                on the cusp
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                Susan Grigsby
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              are abusing a population, and those who, by their purchases, are enabling the abusers.
             </p>
             <p>
              Slavery was not "a division of labor" - the discomfort produced in American audiences by the movie "12 years a slave" reminds us viscerally that this was an unspeakably brutal institution administered by people not at all equivalent to those buying a cheaper shirt in Manchester, New Hampshire or Manchester, England.
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
             </p>
             <p class="cb">
              by
              <a href="/user/uid:222645">
               islandchris
              </a>
              on
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54551214#c92">

              </a>
             </p>

              <a href="#c15">

          <ul class="cm i1" id="k54551214">
           <li id="c98">
            <div class="cx" id="cx54551293">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               If it pleases you to think this, ookay
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54551293#c98?mode=alone;showrate=1#c98">
                5+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:329101">
                 kmfmstar
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
                 Skippah
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:184923">
                 La Gitane
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:24566">
                 farmerhunt
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:981231">
                 GreenInCalif
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               The end purchasers of goods may not fully know the level of exploitation and abuse involved in producing them, but the producers along the supply chain certainly do. I wasn't blaming the people who bought the finished clothes so much as the owners of the textile mills in Manchester, England who bought cheap slave-picked and processed cotton and knew it. If you enable evil in a way that's avoidable, you are evil, in your own way.
              </p>
              <p>
               The equivalent today is Apple and Nike.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
               "We have it in our power to begin the world over again." - Thomas Paine, Common Sense, 1776
              </p>
              <p class="cb">
               by
               <a href="/user/uid:65471">
                kovie
               </a>
               on
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54551293#c98">

               </a>

           <li id="c192">
            <div class="cx" id="cx54556552">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               My friend, just because you are but a distant link
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54556552#c192?mode=alone;showrate=1#c192">
                1+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:1015193">
                 Katikam
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               in the chain of abuse, does not shield you from the fact that you
               <em>
                are a link
               </em>
               . It is a matter of proximity, and that is all it is. Imagine that you are on an imaginary "consumer tour" whereupon you are directly on the scene, observing, lashes and all, as the cotton is grown and picked, then hauled and loaded by slaves, to whatever processing and manufacturing is done to your shirt (child labor in Bangladesh perhaps???), and then on to your soft, perfumed shoulders. There are moral wrinkles in your view that only get ironed out by your distance from the history of the shirt.
              </p>
              <p>
               Whenever I must fill the fuel tank of a machine that I deem necessary to run on my organic farm, or to fill my truck, I recite this reminder to myself; "Remember, there's a bucket of blood in every tankful, just as there's a spoonful in every bite of food." I just don't delude myself into the fantasy that my slate is any cleaner than others in the chain.
              </p>
              <p>
               <em>
                "If it ain't local, it ain't organic."
               </em>
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
              </p>
              <p class="cb">

         <li id="c72">
          <div class="cx" id="cx54550839">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Scotland abolished slavery in 1777.
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54550839#c72?mode=alone;showrate=1#c72">
              15+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:15299">
               Woody
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:161474">
               Susan Grigsby
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:202120">
               whaddaya
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:107837">
               on the cusp
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:17483">
               ssgbryan
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
               AnacharsisClootz
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:231987">
               Eric Nelson
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:175978">
               annieli
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:148551">
               mkor7
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:13718">
               Mnemosyne
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:309984">
               Abelia
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:19705">
               susans
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1029120">
               august88
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
               Skippah
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:985214">
               chrisculpepper
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             The Act of Union of 1707 allowed Scotland to retain its own civil law, which was based on the French system.
            </p>
            <p>
             In the Joseph Knight case of 1777, the Scottish Court of Session (effectively their supreme court) ruled slavery to be against the law in Scotland. Knight was an African-born man who had been enslaved and sold in Jamaica, and then brought to Scotland in 1769.
            </p>
            <p>
             Even earlier, the English Chief Justice, Lord Mansfield, had given his opinion that slavery was contrary to the laws of England, although his opinion was not legally binding.
            </p>
            <p>
             All this can be found in Arthur Herman's book
             <em>
              How the Scots Invented the Modern World
             </em>
             , pp 104-05.
            </p>
            <p>
             It's widely taught outside North America that the American Revolution was, in part, fought to retain the right to keep slaves. The opinion and the decision above were certainly straws in the wind that Britain would eventually abolish slavery throughout the Empire, something that most white Americans would have vigorously opposed.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             -8.38, -7.74 My friends, love is better than anger. Hope is better than fear. Optimism is better than despair. So let us be loving, hopeful and optimistic. And well change the world. - Jack Layton
            </p>
            <p class="cb">
             by
             <a href="/user/uid:104570">
              Wreck Smurfy
             </a>
             on
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54550839#c72">

           </div>
          </div>
         </li>
         <li id="c85">
          <div class="cx" id="cx54551129">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             If the Napoleonic War had not been in full swing
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54551129#c85?mode=alone;showrate=1#c85">
              20+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:107837">
               on the cusp
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:231987">
               Eric Nelson
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:148551">
               mkor7
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:104570">
               Wreck Smurfy
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:13718">
               Mnemosyne
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1071941">
               kay3295
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:73782">
               twigg
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:3037">
               Phoenix Woman
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:123187">
               LarryNM
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:62874">
               mightymouse
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:161474">
               Susan Grigsby
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:19705">
               susans
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:217457">
               Laurel in CA
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
               Skippah
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:184923">
               La Gitane
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:170420">
               DrTerwilliker
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1077755">
               Joni DaNerd
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:81763">
               Eikyu Saha
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:985214">
               chrisculpepper
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:9078">
               delbert
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             it is quite likely that Britain would have moved earlier than 1807 to abolish the slave trade and 1833 to abolish of slavery throughout the empire. Of course, by setting a timetable for abolition in the Caribbean it the death knell of the institution had already been delivered. In the US a very different story was developing, and watching John Ridley (historian and script writer for "12 years a slave") being interviewed was the first time I encountered the notion that slavery in America was a growing institution leading into the Civil War.
            </p>
            <p>
             The thing that continues to astonish me - and I believe is at the heart of a long running American dysfunction - is how untroubled the vast majority of Americans were by the knowledge of millions of slaves living under the lash in their own country. That Americans in Northern states "knew" what was going on was evident in the draft riots that followed the Emancipation Declaration. Many Northerners were hostile to the idea of fighting for black people that they clearly preferred to remain enslaved rather then become competition for jobs in the North.
            </p>
            <p>
             The Emancipation Proclamation was actually rushed out in preliminary form in 1862 to head off British and French recognition of the South, since the overwhelming sentiment of the voting classes in both countries was adamantly opposed to slavery.
            </p>
            <p>
             Black Americans continue to struggle to be accepted as equal citizens to the present day. A majority of white Americans blithely accept the outrageous incarceration rates of young black males; the ease and willingness of police forces or vigilantes to shoot young black men dead; barely disguised racist reasons for defunding welfare; and to seal the deal - accepting lifetime bans on political participation for felons.
            </p>
            <p>
             Never mind the Economist's faint heart - Ferguson is just the latest wakeup call to an honest conversation about a dark cloud over this country that's been there a long time.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
            </p>
            <p class="cb">
             by
             <a href="/user/uid:222645">
              islandchris
             </a>
             on
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54551129#c85">
              Sun Sep 21, 2014 at 03:33:57 PM PDT
             </a>
            </p>

             <a href="#c5">

            </p>

             </p>

              <span class="cu">
               Since you bring up the Caribbean,
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54556697#c194?mode=alone;showrate=1#c194">

             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               note that the nation of Haiti was born of the only successful slave revolt in the Western Hemisphere. It was against the French slaveholders rather than English or American ones, but it scared the bejesus out of Europe and the USA. Consequently, Haiti suffered embargoes that make the sanctions on Cuba, South Africa, Iran etc. look like slaps on the wrist. No wonder it's the poorest country in the West today.
              </p>

             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               was no pipe dream. It continued as a labor-camp state for several decades after the 1898~ Spanish-American War (under US tutelage instead of Spanish). Too late to thwart abolitionists, and it probably didn't satisfy anyone longing for Dixie, but the debt remains.

          <li id="c197">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54557018">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Restitution and the Dutch in Indonesia
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54557018#c197?mode=alone;showrate=1#c197">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:985214">
                chrisculpepper
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              This summer I was in Amsterdam and tried to track down something I had read long ago in a novel by Pramoedya Toer, to the effect that a Dutch political activist had in the late 19th century calculated the enormity of the monetary debt that the Dutch owed to their slave plantations in Indonesia. The staff at the Colonial Museum (Tropensmuseum) and the Multatuli museum were immensely supportive but ultimately not much help. A decent history of Dutch anti-slavery activism apparently has yet to be written to explain the changes from the Culture System to the Liberal Policy to the Ethical Policy. The calculations may have been made by Walter Robert van Hovell in the 1860s (the Dutch were very aware of slavery issues in the US), or the whole thing may have been made up by Toer (not likely, although Suharto did have Toer's notes burned so that Toer had to rewrite almost everything from scratch).
             </p>
             <p>
              If anyone has any good leads, please let me know.
             </p>
            </div>

          <div class="ct">

          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            And God said, "Let there be light"; and with a Big Bang, there was light. And God said "Ow! Ow My eyes!" and in a flash God separated light from darkness. "Whew! Now that's better. Now where was I. Oh yea . . ."
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:145933">
             Pale Jenova
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54550282#c8">

           </p>
           <p class="cl">
           </p>
          </div>
         </div>
        </li>
        <ul class="cm i1" id="k54550282">
         <li id="c47">
          <div class="cx" id="cx54550578">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             The 19th century had three important features:
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54550578#c47?mode=alone;showrate=1#c47">
              11+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
               AnacharsisClootz
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:231612">
               lorikristynmattjulie
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:45368">
               historys mysteries
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:984356">
               hbk
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:104570">
               Wreck Smurfy
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:112805">
               NotGeorgeWill
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:3037">
               Phoenix Woman
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:145933">
               Pale Jenova
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
               Skippah
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:985214">
               chrisculpepper
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:81763">
               Eikyu Saha
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             A. Steam power
            </p>
            <p>
             B. Capitalists who amassed wealth from the trade of slave-produced goods, but who lived in places where there were no slaves.
            </p>
            <p>
             C. Independent freeholding farmers who could make use of the industrial output -- whether the advanced non-powered implements, or the steam-powered stuff like threshers.
            </p>
            <p>
             Between these three, the stage was set for the IndRev.
            </p>
            <p>
             Manual labor
             <i>
              might
             </i>
             have been the most efficient way to plant, tend, and pick cotton in 1850 (or at least, efficient enough to discourage investment in automation) but it certainly wouldn't have been as efficient for harvesting and threshing wheat. The existence of the independent small freeholder in the non-slave north guaranteed that the surplus wealth created by southern slaves would be invested in the development of northern industries.
            </p>
            <p>
             At any rate, the conventional wisdom is that what actually triggered America's industrial explosion wasn't the financial wealth produced from cotton, but rather the spectacular deficit spending of the Civil War.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             <em>

              </span>

               As for cotton, it was cheap and available in India and parts of the Middle East in Hellenistic times (which is also when Hero of Alexandria made his little steam engine), but it was all but unknown in the Mediterranean. Linen, wool, and silk were common and cheap, though.

            <p>
             David Swanson, who with Nicholson "
             <a href="http://www.thenation.com/article/blowing-smoke">
              Human Smoke
             </a>
             " Baker is one of the better-known antiwar activists in America, argued that since the US was the only nation to ever fight a war to end slavery within its borders, that meant that
             <a href="http://my.firedoglake.com/davidswanson/2011/04/11/lies-about-the-u-s-civil-war-150-years-later/">
              the war was unnecessary
             </a>
             as slavery would have died off anyway:
             <br/>
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             Regardless of who said what when, the war did end slavery and was therefore justifiable. Or so were told. Yet, every other nation that ended slavery did so without a civil war. Similarly, we justify the American war for independence because it brought independence, even though Canada and countless other countries achieved independence without war. If we had used a war to create public schools, we would denounce critics of that war as opponents of education. To seriously justify a war, however, would require showing that anything it accomplished could not have been accomplished without all the killing, wounding, traumatizing, and destroying. What if the North had allowed the South to secede and repealed the fugitive slave law? What if an independent North had used trade, diplomacy, and morality to pressure the South to end slavery? Would slavery have lasted longer than the Civil War raged? If so, we are still talking, at best, about a war to hasten the end of slavery.
             <p>
              [...]
             </p>
             <p>
              Secession has as bad a name as socialism, but Wisconsin could secede, ban foreign (U.S.) money from its elections and create a government of, by, and for the people by next year. A seceded California could be one of the most pleasant nations to live in on earth. Vermont would have a civilized healthcare system already if not for Washington, D.C. Yes, the North helped end Jim Crow in the South, but the South did most of that on its own, and we all helped end Apartheid in South Africa without being South Africa. In the absence of viable representative government, we wont do much else on a national scale that we can be proud of. We now, in the United States, imprison more people of African descent than were enslaved here at the time of the Civil War, and it is national policies, completely out of the control of the American people, that produce that mass incarceration.
             </p>
            </blockquote>
            And yes, none of his recent ancestors were slaves.

             <span class="cu">
              The only problem is, as Baptist writes, there is
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54552516#c146?mode=alone;showrate=1#c146">
               4+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:19705">

            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              simply no indication that slavery would have died off on its own.
             </p>
             <p>
              As long as the South had a stranglehold on the Congress, which it did, there was no way that a political solution could have been achieved. Baptist reports that there was even a "gag rule" that prevented the abolitionists voices from being heard in Congress. Between the enslavers in the South and the wealthy in the North, whose wealth was directly tied to the rising price of cotton, abolition of slavery through legislation was not going to happen.
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
              <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/user/Coachella%20Kossacks" rel="nofollow">

              <p>
               Whether or not slavery would have eventually died off on it's own, I would say it's perfectly all right to burn a continent to ash to lessen the total time of the existence of such a practice by one second, and I'm not being snarky or ironic. It's something that never should have happened in the first place. It's something that never should have been allowed to continue the moment it started, and beheading the bastards that tried to keep "their" slaves was an entirely appropriate response.
              </p>

               You try to enslave people, then you don't get to bitch when they burn your world down. You burned their world down first.

           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54550288#c10?mode=alone;showrate=1#c10">
             6+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
              AnacharsisClootz
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:142414">
              JG in MD
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:88267">
              jessical
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:3037">
              Phoenix Woman
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:83832">
              OHdog
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
              Skippah
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Look, people pursue the Eugene Genovese argument with a kind of vigor that betrays too much certainty.
           </p>
           <p>
            Genovese (
            <em>
             The Political Economy of Slavery
            </em>
            ) made the Southern Apologists seem legitimate by agreeing that an untrained, immobile labor force,
            <i>
             in the conditions that existed after the Great Compromise
            </i>
            , was creating an economically unsustainable burden on the southern economy. Slavery was economically unsustainable, because it had a labor force that was kept from being able to adapt to any price shock.
           </p>
           <p>
            Upon his head now dance legions of people smugly saying, "Nunh-unh!"
           </p>
           <p>
            It's important to be clear: if you take numbers from 1848 - 1860, slavery is going to look like a viable economic model, because that's the boom as well as the beginning of a possible bust. However, the south's secession was triggered by an inability to deal with a tariff on cotton. There hadn't been any bills to abolish slavery. This argues that the margins were small enough (largely due to debts to speculators and banking concerns to purchase slaves), and the crop itself susceptible enough to weather and disease, that having a labor force that could not adapt (on purpose) and having only a small band of people with very high profits was already putting an economic strain on the region.
           </p>
           <p>
            The southern apologist's arguments are a
            <em>
             non sequitur
            </em>
            . We don't have to debate them, because they were irrational. After all, no one can say that, because
            <i>
             cotton
            </i>
            was turning into a bubble and the massive expansion of slavery depended upon that rapid capital inflation, the popping of the bubble would have eliminated slavery. No one can say that the pre-Whitney slavery conditions would have moved toward wage work. No one, in short, can agree with the southern apologists, but let's at least be clear with our terms. Cotton, slavery, plantation, and the south are all distinct from one another.
           </p>
           <p>
            Alright. I'll put on the blindfold and stand still now. I know what happens next.
           </p>
          </div>

            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              The tariff was THE issue.
             </p>
             <p>
              Yes, the southern states all talked about slavery in the declarations, but read the whole documents. The primary reference is Genovese. The book is still in print, but. . . honestly, if you are even slightly familiar with the events precipitating the Civil War you know about the tariff on cotton.
             </p>
             <p>
              It's amazing to me that people are now completely informed of a piece of a counter-argument ("They did too secede over slavery") to an argument ("It was states' rights and nothing else") that was a counter to the standard argument ("It was a tariff that threatened trade with England so that cotton would sell more cheaply in northern markets") and have not even heard the base argument ("northern states imposed tariffs to increase supplies in their textile mills and decrease re-importation from the UK, and southern states perceived this as a way of driving their people of wealth out of business").
             </p>
             <p>
              It's not hard to understand. Just remember, "It's one nation, not two, and rich people are greedy."
             </p>

              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                The battles in Kansas had a lot to do with the battles in Missouri, and I'm not convinced that the violence can be attributed to the east coast political issues very clearly when other factors mixed with revenge killings and western politics of territorial expansion. The agents of violence in turn had sponsors from northeast and southeast trying to gain or keep congressional and presidential majorities.
               </p>
               <p>
                The idea that there was a South that had a separate political will from a North is rooted in slavery, of course, as well as immigration patterns. There is no doubt that slave holding gave the otherwise fractious southern states a set of common issues, and it also gave them a homogenous agricultural economy. On the frontiers, though, political radicals and the dispossessed met in a very hot environment.

               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:3037">
                 Phoenix Woman
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
                 Skippah
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:309636">
                 The Geogre
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               economic speculation in mortgages taken out on African American slaves.
              </p>
              <p>
               Consolidated Association of the Planters of Louisiana (C.A.P.L.) was chartered by the state in 1827
              </p>
              <blockquote>
               Here are the nuts and bolts of the C.A.P.L. First, potential borrowers would apply to buy stock in the Association. Their application accepted, they could mortgage slaves and land to the C.A.P.L. in order to pay for the stock. The stock would entitle them to borrow C.A.P.L. bank notes of up to half the value of the mortgaged property. To ensure that people would take these bank notes at face value, the founders needed a large reserve of hard cash. They planned to raise it by selling bonds on the financial markets of the Western world. Each bond would be $ 500 in face value about the average price, in the 1820s, of a young enslaved man. A bond would reach maturity in ten to fifteen years , and it would pay investors 5 percent in annual interest.
               <p>
                Lenders always want security, though, so how would the C.A.P.L. assure potential investors that the bonds would be worth their face value plus interest? Thomas Baring of Baring Brothers helped Lavergne and Forstall to convince the state legislature to back the C.A.P.L.s bonds with the faith and credit of Louisiana . If loan repayments from planters failed and the bank could not pay off the bonds, the taxpayers of Louisiana were now obligated to do so.
               </p>
              </blockquote>
              The bonds were eagerly bought up by investors.
              <br/>
              <blockquote>
               The financial product that such banks as Baring Brothers were selling to investors in London, Hamburg, Amsterdam, Paris, Philadelphia, Boston, and New York was remarkably similar to the securitized bonds, backed by mortgages on US homes, that attracted investors from around the globe to US financial markets from the 1980s until the economic collapse of 2008. Like the C.A.P.L. bonds, mortgage-backed securities shifted risk away from the immediate originators of loans onto financial markets while promising to spread out and thus minimize the consequences of individual debtors failures.
              </blockquote>
              Baptist, Edward E. (2014-09-09). The Half Has Never Been Told: Slavery and the Making of American Capitalism (Kindle Locations 5304-5308). Basic Books. Kindle Edition.
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
               <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/user/Coachella%20Kossacks" rel="nofollow">
                Coachella Kossacks
               </a>
              </p>

           <li id="c196">
            <div class="cx" id="cx54556831">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               You're on your way to revealing capitalism
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54556831#c196?mode=alone;showrate=1#c196">
                2+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:309636">
                 The Geogre
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:235580">
                 a2nite
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               as an abstraction of the feudal system. The lord of the manor in his castle didn't take money from his serfs. He took a share of the fruits of their labor in return for his contribution of the right to use his land (i.e. his capital). There was social inequality and a class system imposed by force, but the system was pretty stable until it was undermined by the "money system" (as my sister calls it). Money, be it gold or paper, is fungible whereas real estate certainly isn't.
              </p>
              <p>
               There's the root of the problem. It's not "the love of money" i.e. greed. It's the abstraction that allows us to enjoy the fruits of other people's labor without having to face how it was beaten out of them. It's the abstraction that allows us to enjoy our delicious In-N-Out burger for less than $5 without having to face the damage done to our ecosystems in bringing it to us.
              </p>
              <p>
               Ironically, In-N-Out is about the only fast-food chain that pays a living wage.
              </p>
             </div>

              <p class="sig">

          <li id="c186">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54554864">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Some Northerners were well aware of this
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54554864#c186?mode=alone;showrate=1#c186">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                Susan Grigsby
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              It's why there was actually an attempt to create a native silk industry in Massachusetts and other areas in the early 19th century. Silk was made with non-slave labor, which is why silk was popular with abolitionists and free thinkers. There was even a commune near Northampton devoted to sericulture, with its most prominent member being Sojourner Truth.
             </p>
             <p>
              These efforts pretty much all failed because, like so many modern progressives, the abolitionists behind "The Community" in what is now Florence, Massachusetts, had no idea of how to run a business. Trained silkmen like William Skinner, Edward Vallentine, and Alfred Lilly eventually came in, took over the businesses, and established what came to be a thriving silk industry later in the century, with two of the businesses they founded (Corticelli Thread and Skinner &amp; Sons Silk) surviving into the 20th century. All of these men were politically pretty advanced for their time, with several strong abolitionists and at least one female suffrage supporter among them.
             </p>
            </div>

           <div class="cx" id="cx54552431">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              The question in re automation
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54552431#c143?mode=alone;showrate=1#c143">
               4+ / 0-

            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              What Genovese argued is that the chattel slavery of the south demanded that the slaves be kept
              <em>
               illiterate
              </em>
              . Genovese did not argue, as far as I recall, that the slave holders needed to create a cultural image of the child-like slave or the animalistic African, but many historians have documented those efforts. Slave holders argued that Africans were incapable of intelligence, happiest when doing labor, in need of close monitoring because of their uncontrollable lusts and violence, and that they were improved by the vicious treatment on the plantation.
             </p>
             <p>
              There were very few slaves in the cities of the south, he argued. While slaves
              <em>
               could
              </em>
              have been used in retail or wholesale stores, they very rarely were. This was due to the slave holder's psychological need to believe that the African slave was incapable.
             </p>
             <p>
              In other words, to sleep at night and believe in the Bible, the slave holders had to convince themselves that their work force was no better than an animal, and that meant that the economy would be stagnant or destroyed by its base. That was his position. I can't agree with his conclusion, because "had to" and "would" are predictions, and the very strength of the hateful stereotypes could have effectively meant never getting rid of slavery.
             </p>
             <p>
              After all, slavery is gone, but the hideous monster of the slave holder's propaganda survives to this day.
             </p>

               It was tobacco, but as land to the west of the coastal plain opened up, cotton was planted where the soil was better.

         <li id="c79">

           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             The slavery issue was the central . . .
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54551007#c79?mode=alone;showrate=1#c79">
              4+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:62874">
               mightymouse

           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             issue of the 1860 campaign. The question of slavery expansion in the 15 years leading up to the 1860 election infected every policy debate -- including debates over trade and tariffs.
            </p>
            <p>
             Lincoln talked about gradual emancipation -- taking place over 100 years. Even this was too much for the Southern slaveholders. None of the southern states seceded because of Lincoln's stance on tariff and trade policies. They objected to his stance on limiting the westward expansion of slavery, because they understood that that would likely be the death knell of slavery over time.
            </p>
            <p>
             It's telling that in the 1790s you have people like Washington and Jefferson expressing moral reservations about the practice of slavery. Those voices weren't very loud by the 1860s. Calhoun replaced Clay as the leading southern statesmen in the decades after the founding and that transition was reflected in the hardline that secessionist took.
            </p>

         <li id="c129">
          <div class="cx" id="cx54552159">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Small details in history often prove to be
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54552159#c129?mode=alone;showrate=1#c129">
              3+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:309636">
               The Geogre
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
               Skippah
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:161474">
               Susan Grigsby
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             momentous in reality.
            </p>
            <p>
             The Geogre wrote:
             <br/>
             "No one can say that the pre-Whitney slavery conditions would have moved toward wage work."
            </p>
            <p>
             In actuality without the invention and widespread application of Eli Whitney's cotton gin the southern experiment with cotton production would have been a financial failure. The southern expectation that the traditional colonial agricultural product of tobacco for international export proved to limited at best by the late 1700's and many slave owning planters started experimenting with alternate crops to avoid bankruptcy. Planters in South Carolina noticed that slaves were cultivating a form of rice for their own consumption which mirrored the rice grown in their African homelands. After experimenting with the use of rice in their own meals many South Carolina planters replaced their tobacco crops with rice crops. It is noted that the land and the weather of South Carolina and its islands were uniquely adaptable to growing rice, which was not available to other Southern planters.
            </p>
            <p>
             However the majority of southern planters decided to experiment with growing cotton crops. However production problems soon showed up in the process of removing the seeds from the cotton blossom itself, a step that was critical for expanding plantation outputs to satisfy industrial levels. Planters found that the addition of multiple slave hands to this one very difficult task made absolutely no difference in providing any significant increase in output. Frustrated southern planters in desperation widely advertised for any invention that could be used to solve this vexing problem in cotton production.
            </p>
            <p>
             A slave owned by Eli Whitney ( we will never know his name because this part of history was written by white men) heard about the cotton planter's appeal and thinking that if he (the slave) could create such an invention, Whitney would gratefully give him his freedom. The slave worked long hours on his own free time in a shed on Whitney's farm, and eventually fabricated (using his own hand made tools) the ubiquitous famous cotton gin. After Whitney's slave showed and demonstrated the cotton ginning machine he had invented, Eli Whitney immediately engaged a patent attorney and with some preliminary drawings he set out to sell the gin to the Southern planters, and the rest is history. Eli Whitney revealed the fact that his slave had invented the cotton gin as part of his death bed confession. Whitney's remorse was not over his stealing his slaves invention, but over the fact that he did not grant his faithful slave his long desired freedom during the slave's lifetime. Whitney felt as did his fellow slaveholders that all possessions of one's slave belonged to the slave owner, as the slave was that persons property.
            </p>
            <p>
             In summary the odds are that without the cotton gin, Southern planters would have never been able to scale up cotton production to capture 61% of the world market for cotton, and hence the influx of wealth to every sector of America , north, east, and south from the American cotton industry would have never materialized. Further the value of the slaves held in the United States just before the Civil War would not have reached its estimated trillion dollar level either.
            </p>
            <p>
            </p>
           </div>

            My biggest fear

            <a href="/comments/1330771/54550344#c16?mode=alone;showrate=1#c16">

             </a>

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            When Marxist historians strip away the language of cultural desire to examine the underlying economics of the base condition, we frequently come to shocking truths. One of these is that America's prosperity 1685 - 1875 was due to a simple, ugly coalition of state violence, forced labor, and uncontrolled speculative markets. When we do this, we recognize that Andrew Jackson's Indian removals, New England shipping, and southern slave holders were a single entity -- neither morally nor politically separable from one another. It's an analysis that is supposed to allow us to see how the base creates the ideology, the ideology creates the superstructure.
           </p>
           <p>
            What I fear most is that today's reactionaries take our analyses and embrace them. Freed of morality, and rejecting the very idea of history, they claim exactly as Hitler did, that forced labor is "natural" for some people, that ancestry or the right or nature determines the best way of doing things, that they will advocate as policy crimes that we diagnose in history.
           </p>
           <p>
            They've done it before. This, I think, is what it means that history repeats once as tragedy, then as farce.
           </p>

              <span class="cu">
               Once we had a choice, I think
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54552533#c148?mode=alone;showrate=1#c148">
                3+ / 0-
               </a>

               </dt>

             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               I seem to remember, in an era. . . I think it was called "before 1992". . . long ago, that it was possible to avoid things made in China or in places where gets a bit of guilt with the gilt. Now, though, asking for a union label or something made in America gets something from Taipei, at best.
              </p>
              <p>
               I want to get my dog rawhide to chew. You know. . . cow bits that aren't in my burger. If I go to Mal-Wart,
               <b>
                every
               </b>
               product there is "made in China." Think of the voyage that cow hide had to take. I'm pretty sure that dead Chinese cows don't charge more than dead American or Mexican cows. If I want a squeaky toy,
               <b>
                every
               </b>
               option, even though labeled Hartz, is made in China. If I get clothing, I can only choose which underpaid laborers I think might be exploited less.
              </p>

           </span>

            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54550352#c18?mode=alone;showrate=1#c18">
             6+ / 0-
            </a>

And England wanted to be fed corn.

           <p>
            that the founders that liberals most often live to praise for their defense of freedom, like Jefferson, Madison and Henry, were unrepentant southern slave owners, while those we like to condemn, conservatives like Hamilton and Adams, were adamantly anti-slavery, and tried their best to end it.
           </p>
           <p>
            Which is why I've never fully bought into the left-right divide. It was the GOP, of course, that was principally founded on a free soil and anti-slavery basis, while the Democratic party was largely pro-slavery and dominated by southern racists until well into the 20th century. Progressive Republicans have since morphed into liberal Democrats and racist Democrats became conservative Republicans. And still we fight over race and race-based issues like poverty, education, jobs, education and housing.
           </p>
           <p>
            We still have slavery, just in another form. And we should be careful who we call our friends and enemies. Neoliberalism is just another form of slavery, with somewhat more freedom and choice, and those who support it are advocating for policies not entirely unlike slavery. The economics of racism and the racism of economics is inescapable. Slavery is economics made to serve one group's interests. Just with a fancier name and fewer whippings.
           </p>
           <p>
            Note that I'm not diminishing the deep suffering experienced by actual slaves nor saying that working 60 hours a week at $8/hr is the same thing as actual slavery. But exploitation is exploitation and its all in the same broad category, made possible by disproportionate power and money held and abused by a few over the many using an unfair economic system.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            "We have it in our power to begin the world over again." - Thomas Paine, Common Sense, 1776
           </p>

              He owned and lived in the Grange for just a few years. It was fairly small, hardly an estate. Are you referring to his wife's family's estate in upstate NY? In any case, he never made money off whatever black workers he had, treated them well from what I understand, and given what I know about him I find it hard to believe that he never paid them. And his anti-slavery stance was hardly merely theoretical. He help found one of the first abolition societies in the US, tried to free slaves during the war to fight the British, and agitated politically to end slavery. But in that era, he was fighting an impossible battle.
             </p>
             <p>
              As for Adams, many on the left condemn him not only for the A&amp;S Acts, and rightly so, but for his overall conservatism, for the same reasons they condemn Hamilton, e.g. that they were supposedly royalists, that they only cared about the upper class, that they implemented financial policies that enriched only a few, that they were imperialists, etc. Never mind that they and the Federalists put the country on a sound economic and political founding that made it possible for the new republic to survive and eventually thrive.
             </p>

               <li id="c181">

                 <h5>
                  <a class="de">
                  </a>
                  <span class="cu">
                   There was actually some discussion
                  </span>
                  <span class="crd ntb">
                   (
                   <a href="/comments/1330771/54554329#c181?mode=alone;showrate=1#c181">
                    0+ / 0-
                   </a>

                  </span>
                 </h5>
                 <div class="ct">
                  <p>
                   even among some southern slave owners about the possibility of freeing slaves. But when they realized that most southern leaders would never accept that, that the southern economy would suffer, that it would lead to massive social dislocation, and that they would personally lose a lot of money, they shelved it. They were probably never that serious about it, and considered it more to sooth their consciences and look good to posterity than because they actually intended and expected to free the slaves.
                  </p>
                  <p>
                   But the efforts of some northerners to free slaves were sincere and intense, even if they too eventually gave up, realizing that there would literally be no US unless they did. It was a horrible bargain, but an unavoidable one. Either a split US, or a US in which slavery was allowed. We all know how that turned out. But I don't see how it could have gone differently. Either way, there was going to be slavery in the former colonies. Only war would end that.
                  </p>

             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
          </li>
          <ul class="cm i1" id="k54551001">
           <li id="c99">
            <div class="cx" id="cx54551299">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               Ah, here is the mistake so many of us make.
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1330771/54551299#c99?mode=alone;showrate=1#c99">
                8+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
                 AnacharsisClootz
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:38543">
                 Diana in NoVa
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:235086">
                 Yahzi
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:73782">
                 twigg
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:62874">
                 mightymouse
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:231733">
                 rexxnyc
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:3037">
                 Phoenix Woman
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:1027885">
                 Skippah
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               Where do we get the money and who should we give it to.
              </p>
              <p>
               Let's not get too involved in the mechanics of the dollars before we study the reality of what was involved with American slavery.
              </p>
              <p>
               As a nation, we have never even acknowledged "the fundamental injustice and inhumanity of slavery."
              </p>
              <p>
               Personally, I don't think any amount of money can make up for the lost generations of Americans who labored in those camps. But until we take a hard look at slavery, "its subsequent racial and economic discrimination against freed slaves," and we study "the impact of those forces on today's living African Americans," we have no way of knowing.
              </p>
              <p>
               As a society, we have never taken a look at the cost of the remnants of slavery that were codified into the post Civil War Jim Crow laws. Or the continuing price that African Americans are made to pay under the New Jim Crow laws that feed our home-grown prison industry as a result of the drug wars.
              </p>
              <p>
               The problem with saying we don't have the money is that we close the discussion before we even have it. Let's put the money aside, and have a discussion about slavery and how it continues to impact all Americans.
              </p>
             </div>

              <p class="sig">

               </p>
               <p>
                We could hold hearings, take evidence, reach conclusions and make apologies.
               </p>
               <p>
                We could endow foundations that would invest in education, healthy and housing in the areas where the suffering is still great. We could build new universities and do it all while refusing profit to private companies other than for their labor.
               </p>
               <p>
                We could re-write the text books to reflect more accurately the bloody past, and the unjust present.
               </p>
               <p>
                We could release half of the current prison population to well paid jobs, with good housing, to make this happen.
               </p>
               <p>
                There are a million things we could do, some good ideas, some not so good, but saying "We can't afford it" is just an excuse to do nothing.

And then what about the poor whites, exploited by mining companies and big farmers and all the others malefactors?
            </p>

            </p>

             <em>
              everyone
             </em>
             toward the bottom.
            </p>
            <p>

            </p>
