          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            it - despite its titles claims. The story has been told - but often ignored. The first book I read about Capitalism and Slavery had that title - written by
            <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Capitalism-Slavery-Eric-Williams/dp/0807844888">
             Eric Williams
            </a>
            in 1944. Williams was the prime minister of Trinidad and Tobago from 1961 until he died in 1981. He was also a former professor of poli-sci at Howard University.
           </p>
           <p>
            The Economist' trashing Baptists' work was, as you said - probably good - in that the backlash has provoked discussion of the book.
           </p>
           <p>
            I've ordered it - thanks for writing this - it prodded me to get it sooner, rather than later.

            <p>
             Baptist intentionally chose to avoid the use of the word "plantation" in his work because it evokes the lies we have always been told about the South, in books and films like Gone With the Wind and Birth of a Nation. The reality is that they were slave labor camps. He refers to the owners as "enslavers" quite frequently. And the African Americans were often "enslaved."

            <li id="c96">
             <div class="cx" id="cx54551246">
              <h5>
               <a class="de">
               </a>
               <span class="cu">
                Southern states made it hard to simply free slaves
               </span>
               <span class="crd ntb">
                (
                <a href="/comments/1330771/54551246#c96?mode=alone;showrate=1#c96">
                 6+ / 0-
                </a>
                )
               </span>
              </h5>
              <div class="crs">
               <dl>
                <dt>
                 Recommended by:
                </dt>
                <dd>
                 <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
                  AnacharsisClootz
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:38543">
                  Diana in NoVa
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:19705">
                  susans
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:62874">
                  mightymouse
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                  Susan Grigsby
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:329101">
                  kmfmstar
                 </a>
                </dd>
               </dl>
              </div>
              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                When Edgar Poe inherited a slave, he was forbidden by Virginia law to simply free the person. So he resorted to a maneuver that was popular among persons in his situation, which was to find a free black family and "sell" the person to them for a nominal fee. The family would in turn do something like go on a trip up North and return without the now-former slave.
               </p>
              </div>
              <div class="cf">
               <p class="sig">

               </p>

                <a href="/user/uid:3037">

             <li id="c111">
              <div class="cx" id="cx54551627">
               <h5>
                <a class="de">
                </a>
                <span class="cu">
                 Indeed, Southern society made it hard to free
                </span>
                <span class="crd ntb">
                 (
                 <a href="/comments/1330771/54551627#c111?mode=alone;showrate=1#c111">
                  6+ / 0-
                 </a>
                 )
                </span>
               </h5>
               <div class="crs">
                <dl>
                 <dt>
                  Recommended by:
                 </dt>
                 <dd>
                  <a href="/user/uid:19705">
                   susans
                  </a>
                  ,
                  <a href="/user/uid:3037">
                   Phoenix Woman
                  </a>
                  ,
                  <a href="/user/uid:62874">
                   mightymouse
                  </a>
                  ,
                  <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                   Susan Grigsby
                  </a>
                  ,
                  <a href="/user/uid:329101">
                   kmfmstar
                  </a>
                  ,
                  <a href="/user/uid:142414">
                   JG in MD
                  </a>
                 </dd>
                </dl>
               </div>
               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 slaves. I wrote a
                 <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/02/11/1276726/-Robert-Carter-III-American-History-s-Forgotten-Liberator">
                  diary
                 </a>
                 once about Robert Carter III, who on 5 September 1791 read a proclamation that he would free all 450 of his slaves.
                </p>
                <p>
                 He did it gradually, freeing a certain number at a time until he was sure they could earn a living in the economy. He was so unpopular that his fellow slave labor camp owners threatened to shoot him. In 1793 he had to flee to Baltimore to live with his daughter. He died in 1804.
                </p>
                <p>
                 It is difficult for me to find anything at all to like about the history of the American South.
                </p>

              <li id="c170">
               <div class="cx" id="cx54553372">
                <h5>
                 <a class="de">
                 </a>
                 <span class="cu">
                  A brief synopsis by Henry Louis Gates
                 </span>
                 <span class="crd ntb">
                  (
                  <a href="/comments/1330771/54553372#c170?mode=alone;showrate=1#c170">
                   1+ / 0-
                  </a>
                  )
                 </span>
                </h5>
                <div class="crs">
                 <dl>
                  <dt>
                   Recommended by:
                  </dt>
                  <dd>
                   <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                    Susan Grigsby
                   </a>
                  </dd>
                 </dl>
                </div>
                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  <a href="http://www.theroot.com/articles/history/2013/03/black_slave_owners_did_they_exist.html">
                   http://www.theroot.com/...
                  </a>
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  As of 1830 about 0.6% of African-American slaves were owned by free black slave owners. The total number of black slave owners at the time was 3776, and 42 percent of them owned just one slave, in many cases a family member. At the other end of the spectrum, John Carruthers Stanly (slave-born, free black) owned 3 plantations, hired white overseers, and had 163 slaves.
                 </p>
                </div>
                <div class="cf">
                 <p class="sig">
                  Do you live in SoCal? Connect! Unite! Act! Join Los Angeles Kossacks. I'm in CA-35. What's your district?
                 </p>
                 <p class="cb">
                  by
                  <a href="/user/uid:985442">
                   benamery21
                  </a>
                  on
                  <a href="/comments/1330771/54553372#c170">

            <li id="c119">
             <div class="cx" id="cx54551858">
              <h5>
               <a class="de">
               </a>
               <span class="cu">
                You will start your research from a conclusion?
               </span>
               <span class="crd ntb">
                (
                <a href="/comments/1330771/54551858#c119?mode=alone;showrate=1#c119">
                 2+ / 0-
                </a>
                )
               </span>
              </h5>
              <div class="crs">
               <dl>
                <dt>
                 Recommended by:
                </dt>
                <dd>
                 <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                  Susan Grigsby
                 </a>
                 ,
                 <a href="/user/uid:3037">
                  Phoenix Woman
                 </a>
                </dd>
               </dl>
              </div>
              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                You know, it is entirely possible for your great-grandfather to be a warm, loving father, a good physician, a honest and loyal man, and also own and beat slaves.
               </p>
               <p>
                All that is required to reconcile these facts is for your grandfather to not recognize black people as human.
               </p>
               <p>
                Given the day in which he lived, it is entirely likely that he did not. He probably owned slaves in exactly the same way he owned horses, and for the same reason: they were animals, not people.
               </p>
               <p>
                I don't know why you cannot bear to think of your grandfather holding the same view that his peers held, but it strikes me as completely unnecessary.
               </p>
               <p>
                Yes, it reflects badly on your ancestor that he could stare human suffering in the face and not see it; but measured by that standard, we're not really in a place to judge too harshly. From Rawanda to gay rights, our generation has done plenty of denying too.
               </p>
              </div>
              <div class="cf">
               <p class="sig">
               </p>

            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              your ancestors were "evil."
             </p>
             <p>
              People owned slaves in order to make a profit that they would not have been able to make had they paid a fair wage. I am sure that they did not see themselves as evil. But the consideration of human beings as chattel property, is in itself evil.
             </p>
             <p>
              Any time people are held against their will and forced to labor, the only realistic description of the place in which they are held is a slave labor camp. For too long we have tried to ignore the reality of the cruelty inherent in slavery under the skirts of words like plantations.
             </p>

             Indeed, the fact that there wasn't a middle class
            </span>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             ...in the South (just slaves, poor whites, and a tiny numbers of landowners and slaveowners with the people they employed directly or indirectly) was a contributing factor to its defeat.
            </p>
            <p>
             Not many of the slave camp owners wanted to contribute more than the bare minimum to the Confederate Army, whose troops often didn't even have guns, much less uniforms, a situation that grew more common as the war wore on. They had massive fortunes but were loath to commit much of them.
            </p>
            <p>
             In an interesting contrast, while many wealthy Northern men could and pay people to fight in their stead, there were many who were eager to do their bit for the Union. James J. Hill tried to enlist with the First Minnesota Regiment of Volunteers, and the only reason he couldn't was because of an eye injury he had suffered. (He remained a staunch supporter of the First Minnesota to the end of his life, even helping out First veterans who were down on their luck, which is amazing considering what a cold-hearted prick he was in the business aspects of his life.)

        <li id="c48">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54550584">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            American slavery
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54550584#c48?mode=alone;showrate=1#c48">
             10+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:142414">
              JG in MD
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
              AnacharsisClootz
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:19580">
              88kathy
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:235580">
              a2nite
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:144846">
              dharmasyd
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:1119076">
              lizalice
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:148551">
              mkor7
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:235086">
              Yahzi
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:363836">
              livebyChocolate
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            A quick historical note:
           </p>
           <p>
            Euro-Americans originally enslaved First Nations' peoples, but they kept running away. Then, the Euro-Americans accepted First Nations' "slaves" from other First Nations' peoples, but they kept running off and leaving the slave owners both without "free" labor and poorer as the slave owners paid for these "slaves" in the first place. The First Nations' peoples thought this was great fun, as the escaped "slaves" would come back to their peoples, fight another fight, be resold to the Euro-Americans and a happy cycle was repeated. At one point in American linguistics, the peoples' name Pawnee was synonymous with "slave."
           </p>
           <p>
            The Euro-Americans eventually moved to importing Africans to be used as slave labor as, well, they couldn't run away as easily. But, the First Nations' peoples of the southeastern part of the country did help free these Africans when they could. That is part of what caused so much strife between the Euro-American slave owners and the indigenous peoples upon whose land the Euro-Americans were squatting. And, no one sees the irony of all this?
           </p>
           <p>
            Now, this is a highly abbreviated nugget of American history.
           </p>
           <p>
            I also acknowledge that many indigenous peoples had a sort of slavery. But, here's the main difference between Euro-American slavery and many indigenous peoples' practice of slavery: First Nations' slavery was not an end-all-be-all sort of slavery. It was more of an apprenticeship to learn the new additions to how to be "one of the people." It was an entry level job to inculcate newcomers in how to speak a new language, teach them proper ways of living and respecting new traditions and customs. A slave in a First Nations' tribe could work up the social ladder, marry into the tribe, and eventually become an elder, or other respected member of the community. This is not a universal feature among First Nations' peoples, but it is fairly widespread throughout the indigenous American peoples.
           </p>
           <p>
            Shifting gears to present-day US history:
           </p>
           <p>
            The current form of financial compensation for minimum wage positions is a new form of slavery. It merely allows some employers to assuage their conscience by giving a price to labor. This is because minimum wages do not provide enough financial compensation to allow an individual to live a comfortable life, let alone allow said individual to take a vacation or a sick day, save money for retirement or even afford to buy a house and invest in the American dream of land ownership. The minimum wage barely allows an individual (I say individual rather than a family, because I cannot take the overwhelming feeling of depression when I think about a family of 4 living off of a sole provider earning the minimum wage) to survive, to pay for rent AND food and utilities, let alone maintaining a savings account or any frills like, I dunno, new clothes.
           </p>
           <p>
            Fair market price for physical labor is still terribly underestimated, while the fair market price for inert, inanimate capital is severely overinflated. Until we address the issues at hand, legalized wage slavery will continue to depress our economy. Until we can address both the legal implications of devaluing labor and overvaluing capital, as well as the taxation differences of labor and capital, we will continue to have legalized wage slavery in this country.
           </p>
           <p>
            Until we see humans as valued beings and money as a system of inanimate, inert simplification of the barter system, we will continue to have an imbalance of "wealth" in this culture. Wealth is not necessarily tied to how much inert, inanimate money one has. Wealth can also be measured by our interpersonal connections, our contributions to our communities and our value as animate beings.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:1016753">
             AKBear
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54550584#c48">
             Sun Sep 21, 2014 at 01:53:53 PM PDT
            </a>
           </p>
           <p class="cl">
           </p>
          </div>

           <div class="cx" id="cx54552044">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Least we didn't
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54552044#c126?mode=alone;showrate=1#c126">
               0+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              import a bunch of people from across the ocean for the express purpose of endless, hereditary slavery.
             </p>
             <p>
              Nor did we practice genocide.
             </p>
             <p>
              Nor did we practice persecuting people simply because of whom they loved. Non-heterosexual people were valued for their advice to heterosexual people.
             </p>
             <p>
              Nor did we silence women's voices simply because of their gender.
             </p>
             <p>
              Nor did we cause global pollution to suit our own needs. Sure, we did some regional destruction, but nowhere near the level that, say ExxonMobile, has done.
             </p>
             <p>
              Nor did we impose our religious beliefs on others or beat, maim, rape them into believing in some invisible, intangible sky god who is male. Everyone knows that women bring life into being.
             </p>
             <p>
              Nor did we have standing armies to bludgeon peoples different from us.
             </p>
             <p>
              Shall I go on?
             </p>
             <p>
              You see, it's all a matter of scale. Yeah, we fought, and, yeah, sometimes those we fought died. As a general rule, we didn't go out with massive armies looking for enemies to annihilate. We'd go out, get into skirmishes and count it good if we took something of value from the other guys. It was a game to fill in hunting and gathering and surviving.
             </p>
             <p>
              But, we did not have standing armies to subjugate the peoples next door to us. Yeah, everyone kills -- stories of executions for poor behavior are found throughout the world. We didn't go running willy nilly across the globe telling other peoples that they had to conform to our way or be summarily executed. We did not repress different points of view, but took them under consideration for the best direction to lead the entire community.
             </p>
            </div>

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            as my extremely white, old, Georgia ex "friends" would have me believe that the slaves were taken from the hell hole in Africa where they were terrorized by wild animals, given first class accommodations across the Atlantic, given a nice new white "friend" and protector, a little house with all the appliances they had back then in an idyllic glen, a gentle nudge to get them up in the morning (unless they had a cold or were a bit tired and needed to sleep in), Saturdays and Sundays off with pay, and for those that desired it, a free education.
           </p>
           <p>
            You mean they lied to me???
           </p>

             But even more offensive, to me, than that remark was the one wherein he suggested that the reason for the increase in productivity was because owners considered slaves as an investment and took good care of them.             </p>

         <ul class="cm i1" id="k54552773">
          <li id="c202">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54558419">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Economist Writers are not "anonymous."
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54558419#c202?mode=alone;showrate=1#c202">
               2+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                Susan Grigsby
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:257122">
                belinda ridgewood
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              ...so much as the policy for the magazine has never been to have bylines, for over 160 years. When many other magazines began adding bylines after that time, the Economist simply never did. In other words, there wasn't a single decision to say, "let's keep our writers identities secret!" In fact, there was never a decision at all (the decision would have had to be to ADD bylines), so nothing happened.
             </p>
             <p>
              There are upsides and downsides to this, from the magazine's and the writers' points of views (no big-name celebrity writers who "must" have a cover when there's better stories by younger writers available, nobody's brand eclipses the magazine's, and nobody can be held personally accountable for content except the editorial staff).
             </p>
             <p>
              I'm only putting this out there because we shouldn't read too much into the anonymity of this one review, or really talk about it at all (using the term "anonymous" in the original article was a little charged, and probably distracted from the actual point, which is what we should be talking about). So, to the point  I'll shut up about bylines. Back to the debate!
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
             </p>
             <p class="cb">

           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            It seems to me...
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54551054#c81?mode=alone;showrate=1#c81">
             6+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:153276">
              moviemeister76
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:231987">
              Eric Nelson
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
              AnacharsisClootz
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:62874">
              mightymouse
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:68158">
              edwardssl
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            ...that all the comments comparing slavery to the present day horrors of bitterly low starvation wages in developing countries are kind of missing the point.
           </p>
           <p>
            Modern capitalism is rife with places which give people little choice and where leaving the crappy deal on tap amounts to death. But it is still not the same as being the legally recognized property of another person, who may be killed or raped or whipped or castrated with the full moral and legal approval of society. There are similarities worth pointing out, and moral complicity abounds -- but by saying it is the same we minimize both the present and the past.
           </p>
           <p>
            Mileage varies.
            <br/>
           </p>

             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Although guilty of using the term "wage slaves" in the past, it is unlikely that I will ever do so again. (My original title had a reference to that usage, but it was just too clunky to make a decent title.)
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             But it is still not the same as being the legally recognized property of another person, who may be killed or raped or whipped or castrated with the full moral and legal approval of society. There are similarities worth pointing out, and moral complicity abounds -- but by saying it is the same we minimize both the present and the past.
             <br/>
            </blockquote>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/user/Coachella%20Kossacks" rel="nofollow">
              Coachella Kossacks
             </a>
            </p>

             <a href="/user/uid:161474">

          <li id="c106">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54551422">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              For myself...
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1330771/54551422#c106?mode=alone;showrate=1#c106">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:161474">
                Susan Grigsby
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              ..I am sure I will continue to split hairs that are fine as they are and glue them into a mess when I shouldn't. I tend to think the similarities outweigh the differences in some important ways -- especially when people cannot leave a place and there is nothing else for them, nothing at all, and wages just pay food -- it all comes down to a calculation of death rates against marginal profits -- and good for people for noticing, because part of the way we pretend it is OK is to say well, those people are free to quit and their middle class is growing, so it must be all right. Where I think it breaks down is that it is our great great grandparents who did this, or suffered it, as woven into our vision of the world as alcoholism is wrapped into a dysfunctional family. What you are saying in the diary I think -- all we have, we got in large part from a monstrous crime, and we are living in the echo. Exploitative migration laws and labor practices are a horror, but we are not the overseers of it now, and the children of the victims are (mostly) not our fellow citizens. And we work very, very hard not to hear the echo of slavery in America. It isn't so much that the distinction frees us from responsibility for present abuses as that they have a very different character and impact.
             </p>
             <p>
              Anyway. I felt like I got on a little too high a horse and had to come back :}
             </p>
            </div>

        <li id="c82">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54551089">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Limitations on slavery.
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54551089#c82?mode=alone;showrate=1#c82">
             7+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:153276">
              moviemeister76
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:231987">
              Eric Nelson
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:1039294">
              AnacharsisClootz
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:62874">
              mightymouse
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:15556">
              Bryce in Seattle
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:3037">
              Phoenix Woman
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Part of the challenge with slavery's expansion was that westward expansion wasn't especially favorable to the kind of farming that used slave labor.
           </p>
           <p>
            e.g. the north was growing faster than the population of the south through the aid of immigration, it was more economically productive.
           </p>
           <p>
            In 1860 the writing was on the wall, and this is precisely why the Secessionists broke from the Union not long after Lincoln's election. They weren't going to give up slavery without a fight. The southern ambition was to create a new slave state around the Caribbean -- which was part of the motivation behind the invasion of Mexico in 1846-1848 and calls to push into Cuba.
           </p>
           <p>
            The idea that slavery was going to end naturally within a few decades, wasn't really rational. In terms of power, how often do dictators willingly give up absolute power? In the South you had thousands of small scale dictators who had absolute dominion over their little fiefdoms. They weren't, and didn't give up that power willingly. Even if the slavery system was economically inefficient -- fair arguments in this regard, and the widespread poverty in the South certainly points in this direction -- there are those who would willingly trade greater absolute wealth for more absolute power. We can see this today in a state like North Korea, where slave labor continues to exist. Economically, North Korea would be much wealthier if it opened up its economy and pursued a path more along the lines of South Korea or even a China model which has slave labor along with a rising middle class. Even the Kim family would be much wealthier than it is today if North Korea was an open market economy. But it doesn't because the ruling family doesn't want to give up any power.
           </p>
          </div>

           <p class="sig">
           </p>

            <a href="/comments/1330771/54551089#c82">

            </a>
           </p>

           </p>

        <li id="c102">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54551346">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Marx on Modern Racism &amp; capitalism pre-Civil War
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54551346#c102?mode=alone;showrate=1#c102">
             4+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:175978">
              annieli
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:3037">
              Phoenix Woman
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:62874">
              mightymouse
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            <a href="http://socialistworker.org/2010/10/21/the-roots-of-racism">
             The roots of racism
            </a>
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            Although it is a commonplace for academics and opponents of socialism to claim that Karl Marx ignored racism, Marx in fact described
            <strong>
             the processes that created modern racism. His explanation of the rise of capitalism placed the African slave trade, the European extermination of indigenous people in the Americas and colonialism at its heart
            </strong>
            . In Capital, Marx writes:
            <blockquote>
             The discovery of gold and silver in America, the extirpation, enslavement and entombment in mines of the indigenous population of the continent, the beginnings of the conquest and plunder of India, and the conversion of Africa into a preserve for the commercial hunting of black skins are all things that characterize the dawn of the era of capitalist production.
            </blockquote>
            - emphasis added
           </blockquote>
           <a href="http://images.dailykos.com/images/107008/large/slave_ad1840zz-ad-for-slave-auction-a.jpg?1411339603">
            <img src="http://images.dailykos.com/images/107008/large/slave_ad1840zz-ad-for-slave-auction-a.jpg?1411339603"/>
           </a>
           <blockquote>
            Marx connected his explanation of the role of the slave trade in the rise of capitalism to the social relations that produced racism against Africans. In
            <u>
             Wage Labor and Capital
            </u>
            , written 12 years before the American Civil War, he explains:
            <blockquote>
             What is a Negro slave? A man of the black race. The one explanation is as good as the other.
             <p>
              A Negro is a Negro. He only becomes a slave in certain relations. A cotton spinning jenny is a machine for spinning cotton. It only becomes capital in certain relations. Torn away from these conditions, it is as little capital as gold by itself is money, or as sugar is the price of sugar.
             </p>
            </blockquote>
           </blockquote>
           # # #
           <p>
            It is my belief based on every move the GOP "conservative" movement makes in their so-called fiscal agenda to "loosen" the labor market (with austerity that strangles investment to create and excess labor pool driving wages continually lower) produces, today 2014, the closest equivalent to slave labor that the GOP can get away with.
            <br/>
            And it is with minorities; AA's Latinos, etc. that have the least voice in government that they exploit first using ingrained racist dog whistles to feed the racial divide in this nation to make that happen. But this exploitation includes anyone whenever the GOP can control and get away with it. - so teabags beware
            <br/>
            # # #
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Thx
            </strong>
            Susan Grigsby - for the excellent history on this
           </p>
          </div>

        <li id="c110">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54551574">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            I have some slight disagreements with this
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54551574#c110?mode=alone;showrate=1#c110">
             3+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:62874">
              mightymouse
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:3037">
              Phoenix Woman
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            First, it is wrong to say that slavery flourished in laissez faire capitalism. The slave states had tons of laws protecting slavery. Add to these laws substantially restricting the freedom of "free blacks," They were subject to pass laws whenever they traveled, curfews, work requirements, and other restrictions. As Stephen A. Douglas pointed out in his Freeport Doctrine, slavery could not exist for a day without law enforcing it. This is hardly laissez faire.
           </p>
           <p>
            Second, slavery was dying on the periphery. There were only 5,000 slaves left in Delaware in 1860 and the number of slaves was decreasing in Maryland as tobacco planting in southern Maryland depleted the soil and left vacant farms in their wake. Unfortunately, for the most part the slaves on the closed farms were not freed but instead sold south, which created a glut of slaves and falling prices for slaves on the eve of war.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            "Corporations exist not for themselves, but for the people." Ida Tarbell 1908.
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:54188">
             Navy Vet Terp
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54551574#c110">

            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             It was not dying on the periphery, profits were
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54552842#c158?mode=alone;showrate=1#c158">
              3+ / 0-
             </a>
             )

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             being made by the forced migration of slaves from the old south states like Maryland to the new lands in the new southwest where slavery flourished. There was no glut of slaves, nor were prices falling, according to Baptist, the slaves were just being moved farther west, into Texas and California. It was thriving. Polk tried even during his last few months in office to garner support for the annexation of Cuba.
            </p>
            <p>
             What Baptist does is expose the common wisdom that slavery was inefficient as false.
            </p>

            <span class="cu">
             No.
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1330771/54552885#c160?mode=alone;showrate=1#c160">
              1+ / 0-
             </a>

            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:235580">
               a2nite
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Most of the wealth made by slavery was invested in cities like Lowell Mass, and in the cotton mills of England. It was used to build a railway that joined the west and east coasts of the United States.
            </p>
            <p>
             The profits from slavery went into the pockets of the investors, and they were in the North, the South, England and Europe.
            </p>
            <p>
             England, in addition to investing in slavery, bought cheap cotton produced by slave labor that allowed their mills to create fabric that they could then sell at profit because the raw material was so cheap. They did not pass the savings along to the consumer.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/user/Coachella%20Kossacks" rel="nofollow">
              Coachella Kossacks

        <li id="c164">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54553161">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            The invention of the cotton gin meant that slaves
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54553161#c164?mode=alone;showrate=1#c164">
             1+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            had to produce much more cotton to feed its hungry maw. Before the gin, seeds were picked from the cotton bolls by hand and that was painfully slow. Rising demand for cotton was met by a greater yield due to this invention. So the
            <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotton_gin">
             cotton gin
            </a>
            also increased the demand for field hands.
           </p>
           <p>
            From Wikipedia:
            <br/>
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            Cotton fibers are produced in the seed pods ("bolls") of the cotton plant; as a result, the fibers ("lint") in the bolls are interspersed with the seeds. The seeds must be removed from the lint to make the fibers usable. Historically, this
            <strong>
             task was performed by hand:
            </strong>
            production of cotton
            <strong>
             required hundreds of man-hours to separate the seeds from a useful amount of lint.
            </strong>
            Many simple seed-removing devices have been invented over the years, but until the invention of the cotton gin most required significant operator attention and worked on a small scale.
           </blockquote>
           <em>
            emphasis mine
            <br/>
           </em>
           <blockquote>
            . . . Whitney's gin revolutionised the cotton industry in the United States, but also
            <strong>
             led to the growth of slavery in the American South
            </strong>
            as the demand for cotton workers rapidly increased. The invention has thus been identified as an inadvertent contributing factor to the outbreak of the American Civil War. . . ..
           </blockquote>
           <em>
            emphasis mine
           </em>
           <br/>
           <blockquote>
            The cotton gin thus transformed cotton as a crop and the American South into the globe's first agricultural powerhouse, and  according to many historians  was the start of the Industrial Revolution"
           </blockquote>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            "The object of persecution is persecution. The object of torture is torture. The object of power is power. Now do you begin to understand me?" ~Orwell, "1984"
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:314554">
             Lily O Lady
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54553161#c164">
             Sun Sep 21, 2014 at 08:47:37 PM PDT
            </a>
           </p>
           <p class="cl">

        <li id="c178">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54554013">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            False Contrasts
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54554013#c178?mode=alone;showrate=1#c178">
             1+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:161474">
              Susan Grigsby
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            The review in
            <em>
             The Economist
            </em>
            is also written in bad faith. Baptist's book is contrasted with John Thomas' 1997
            <em>
             The Slave Trade
            </em>
            --an ostensible exemplar of slave scholarship. But
            <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/books/97/11/30/reviews/971130.30thorntt.html">
             here
            </a>
            is what professor John Thornton then had to write in
            <em>
             The New York Times Book Review
            </em>
            of Thomas' book:
            <br/>
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            It is harder to explain why he has also avoided more urgent academic debate. He says this book had its origins 30 years ago, when he met Eric Williams, then Prime Minister of Trinidad and the author of a hugely influential study of the slave trade and its impact on the development of capitalism. Williams upbraided Thomas for not having read his book, ''Capitalism and Slavery,'' and sent him a copy. ''The Slave Trade'' never deals in depth with the central issues of that book, what these days is called ''the Williams thesis,'' which holds that the profitability of the slave trade financed the Industrial Revolution in Europe, and that the growth of industry eventually ended slavery and the slave trade. Debate on the Williams thesis is the cutting edge of slave trade research in academic circles.
           </blockquote>
           It is no wonder
           <em>
            The Economist
           </em>
           looks to Thomas' book as a model of history. It is objective only in the obtuse sense that it eschews moral engagement with the topic and does so in a way that avoids the unpleasantness of slavery's economic impact. In brief, Baptist engages the Williams thesis; Thomas does not.
           <em>
            The Economist
           </em>
           elides this point altogether, suggesting a willful blindness on the part of its reviewer, who either is not familiar with modern slave scholarship or, like Thomas, avoids it altogether for personal and possibly ideological reasons. The bad faith portion of the original review is the obscuring of this ignorance or avoidance by the reviewer's charging Baptist with "not being objective."
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:31231">
             ThinkTank
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54554013#c178">
             Mon Sep 22, 2014 at 05:20:18 AM PDT
            </a>
           </p>
           <p class="cl">
           </p>
          </div>
         </div>
        </li>
        <li id="c193">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54556627">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Slavery is definitely still with us, but
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54556627#c193?mode=alone;showrate=1#c193">
             1+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:989362">
              geniesolver
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            what sustains capitalism today is primarily the exploitation, not of enslaved human beings, but of the global ecosystem. Naomi Klein's new book "This Changes Everything" (subtitled "Capitalism and the Climate") connects the dots. Just as the ongoing brutalization of the slaves forced them to keep increasing their productivity, and thus their owners' profits, so today does the ongoing brutalization of our air, water, land and labor keep increasing the profits of the corporations and the wealth of the capitalists who own them. Technologies such as genetic modification that could be used for the good of humankind are instead developed and promulgated solely for corporate profit. It's the same old story--but maybe now people are starting to pay attention.
           </p>
           <p>
            The hopeful aspect of this diary is that slavery was
            <em>
             not
            </em>
            ended because it was economically outmoded. It became too morally repugnant to be quietly accepted by people outside of its immediate vicinity (who, even if, like most Confederate soldiers, they were not slave owners themselves, had grown up accepting that blacks were subhuman and the "Southern way of life" was honorable rather than despicable).
           </p>
           <p>
            The hope is that enough people in this country and around the world will realize, soon enough, that corporate capitalism, despite having "moved hundreds of millions out of poverty" (the oft-repeated claim), is morally repugnant because it puts the viability of future generations at risk. It must, like slavery, be repudiated not because it isn't working but because it's working far too well.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:981231">
             GreenInCalif
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54556627#c193">
             Mon Sep 22, 2014 at 11:32:17 AM PDT
            </a>
           </p>
           <p class="cl">
           </p>
          </div>
         </div>
        </li>

         <div class="cx" id="cx54557314">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Slavery
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54557314#c200?mode=alone;showrate=1#c200">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            And now slavery is reinvented for capitalist enrichment- forced labour and unjustifiably long sentences in privately run prisons. They finally found the way to get it right again. Sickening
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">

        <li id="c203">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54559705">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            No End to Slavery
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54559705#c203?mode=alone;showrate=1#c203">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Just because we no longer force employment at the end of a whip doesn't mean that slavery no longer exists. It just means that it's covert instead of overt. A much more preferable form of slavery.
           </p>
           <p>
            Let's not forget that the US was not the only country that was awash in slaves.
           </p>

           <p class="sig">

        <li id="c204">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54559935">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Reparations
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54559935#c204?mode=alone;showrate=1#c204">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            <a href="http://www.theatlantic.com/features/archive/2014/05/the-case-for-reparations/361631/">
             why?
            </a>
           </p>
           <p>
            How?
           </p>
           <p>
            John Conyerss HR 40.
           </p>
           <p>
            Let's do it. No more excuses. Nothing less will heal this land.
           </p>
          </div>

        <li id="c205">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54560115">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            The fourteenth amendment which was
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54560115#c205?mode=alone;showrate=1#c205">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            supposed to assure political rights for newly-freed slaves was hijacked by lawyers to assert corporate personhood. Of the 150 cases involving the Fourteenth Amendment heard by the Supreme Court up to the 1896 "separate but equal" decision, 15 involved blacks and 135 involved business entities. Blacks won only one case. Slavery is the legal fiction that a person is property. Corporate personhood is the legal fiction that property is a person.
           </p>
           <p>
            As the 10 million pick up their new iphones remember the Chinese workers building them.... living in company dorms, standing 12 hour shifts at times, exposed to chemicals, low wages. As an employee benefit, the company put nets around the perimeter to catch the suicides.
            <br/>
           </p>

        <li id="c216">
         <div class="cx" id="cx54623376">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Baptists book sounds like a footnote to Marx
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1330771/54623376#c216?mode=alone;showrate=1#c216">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Marx said that in the past slaves (usually prisoners of war) were of the same race and culture as their masters; and in some cases culturally superior to their masters. For example, Roman civilization was built by Greek slaves, many of whom were better educated than their masters; they rose to high positions in Rome, and even owned slaves of their own. Slaves were therefore not considered less than human by their masters, as Americas white slave owners claimed their black African slaves were.
           </p>
           <p>
            Modern race-based slavery made modern capitalism possible because black African slaves were considered property, not people. Slave owners bought them just as they did land, machinery or any other productive property. Marx said the turning of Africa into a warren for the commercial hunting of black skins was the rosy dawn of the era of capitalist production.
           </p>
           <p>
            Im not criticizing Baptists book, which from this review appears to be well researched and written; but I suspect he would have profited from reading what Marx said about American slavery. Few people read Marx nowadays, even those on the left, so they often end up re-inventing the wheel.
            <br/>
           </p>
          </div>

  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
